- use protected content as the core of a competing substitute publication;
- provide unlimited organizational access from an individual license;
- falsely claim publisher endorsement;
- bypass access controls for unauthorized distribution.

Restrictions must be interpreted alongside applicable law and the specific license agreement.

A restriction against redistribution is not a restriction against learning the subject.

A restriction against unauthorized derivative publication is not ownership of general engineering ideas.

---

### 12. LICENSE DURATION

#### 12.1 Personal License Duration

Unless the product description or applicable agreement expressly states otherwise, a Personal License grants perpetual access to the purchased edition and entitled volumes, subject to the license terms and valid enforcement provisions.

The public product description should avoid vague or misleading promises regarding the duration of access.

#### 12.2 Organizational License Duration

Educational Institution, Enterprise, Commercial Training, Library, Government and Public-Sector, subscription, promotional, and other organizational licenses may be:

- perpetual;
- fixed-term;
- renewable;
- subscription-based; or
- otherwise negotiated.

The applicable agreement should clearly define:

- start date;
- end date;
- renewal terms;
- access after expiration;
- archival rights where applicable.

No default duration should be assumed unless expressly specified in the applicable agreement.

#### 12.3 Subscription Access

A subscription grants access according to the subscription terms.

Expiration may end platform access to subscription content.

A subscription should not be marketed as ownership of permanent entitlements unless permanent rights are expressly granted.

#### 12.4 Revocation

A valid license should not be arbitrarily revoked.

Revocation may be considered for serious breach, fraud, chargeback-related entitlement reversal, unauthorized commercial exploitation, or other grounds defined by the applicable terms.

Enforcement, suspension, fines, penalties, and revocation procedures are addressed in later parts of this manual.

---

### 13. LICENSE UPGRADES AND CONVERSIONS

#### 13.1 Purpose

A user's legitimate needs may change.

**Examples:**

- a student graduates;
- an individual creates a company;
- a college adopts a personally owned handbook;
- a trainer begins paid instruction;
- an enterprise expands employee access.

The system should support defined upgrades rather than forcing misuse of an unsuitable license.

#### 13.2 Student to Personal

Previously purchased Student License entitlements may remain valid.

Future purchases may use Personal pricing after student eligibility ends.

A mandatory repurchase of every existing student volume is not the default policy.

#### 13.3 Personal to Enterprise

A Personal License cannot simply be "shared with the company."

The organization should purchase or negotiate Enterprise rights.

The publisher may optionally provide credit or an upgrade path, but this is a commercial policy rather than an automatic right.

#### 13.4 Personal to Commercial Training

A trainer who begins using protected handbook content in paid courses should obtain the appropriate Commercial Training License.

Personal ownership may be considered during commercial negotiation but does not itself authorize training distribution.

#### 13.5 Institution Expansion

An institution may add:

- students;
- campuses;
- instructors;
- handbooks;
- editions.

The licensing system should amend or extend the institutional entitlement rather than creating unclear duplicate records.

#### 13.6 License Downgrade

A license generally does not need to be "downgraded" merely because usage decreases.

A future renewal may use a smaller seat or student allocation if permitted by the agreement.

Previously granted rights remain subject to the applicable terms.

#### 13.7 Conversion Records

Every formal license conversion should record:

- previous license type;
- new license type;
- affected handbooks;
- affected entitlements;
- effective date;
- commercial adjustment;
- reason.

---

### 14. LICENSE SELECTION DECISION FRAMEWORK

Use the following questions:

**Is one individual buying for personal learning or reference?**
→ Personal License.

**Is the individual an eligible verified student buying for themselves?**
→ Student License.

**Is a college, university, or school adopting the handbook for learners?**
→ Educational Institution License.

**Is a company using the handbook internally for employees?**
→ Enterprise License.

**Is protected handbook content used in paid external training?**
→ Commercial Training License.

**Is controlled reference or lending access being provided by a library?**
→ Library License.

**Is a government or public-sector body acquiring organizational rights?**
→ Government/Public-Sector License.

**Is the work being officially translated or localized for publication?**
→ Translation and Localization License.

If more than one use case applies, the publisher should review whether multiple rights must be combined in a special agreement.

**Example:**

A university library provides reference access and a computer science department adopts the handbook for 300 students.

The university may require both library access treatment and institutional student learning rights.

---

### 15. EXCEPTIONS AND SPECIAL LICENSES

The publisher may create special licenses for legitimate use cases not covered by the standard categories.

Possible future examples include:

- reviewer license;
- contributor license;
- scholarship license;
- nonprofit educational license;
- research-group license;
- official partner license;
- accessibility accommodation license;
- press or evaluation license.

A special license should define:

1. authorized party;
2. covered handbook;
3. edition and volumes;
4. permitted use;
5. prohibited use;
6. duration;
7. transferability;
8. termination;
9. entitlement source.

Special licenses should not be used as undocumented verbal exceptions.

---

### 16. FUTURE WEBSITE IMPLEMENTATION GUIDANCE

The future platform should represent license types as structured policy identifiers rather than free-text notes.

**Illustrative identifiers:**

```text
LIC-PERSONAL
LIC-STUDENT
LIC-EDU-INSTITUTION
LIC-ENTERPRISE
LIC-COMMERCIAL-TRAINING
LIC-LIBRARY
LIC-GOVERNMENT
LIC-TRANSLATION
```

A license record should reference:

- License ID;
- license type;
- authorized person or organization;
- Handbook ID;
- edition scope;
- entitlement records;
- status;
- issue date;
- expiration where applicable;
- agreement version;
- special conditions.

The system should support multiple licenses for one customer account.

**Example:**

```text
Customer Account

├── Personal License — Linux Engineering Handbook
├── Personal License — Database Engineering Handbook
└── Enterprise Administrator Role — Company Agreement
```

Account identity and license rights are not the same thing.

The dashboard should explain rights in human-readable language.

Internal codes are useful for software but should not be the only information visible to users.

---

### 17. Decision Summary

1. License type defines permitted use; entitlement defines accessible content.
2. The standard license types are Personal, Student, Educational Institution, Enterprise, Commercial Training, Library, Government/Public-Sector, and Translation/Localization.
3. A Personal License is for one individual's learning and reference.
4. Applying learned knowledge at work does not automatically require an Enterprise License.
5. Organizational sharing of handbook content requires appropriate organizational rights.
6. A Student License remains an individual license and does not permit class-wide sharing.
7. Graduation should not automatically erase previously purchased student entitlements.
8. Institutional licensing uses institutional authorization plus authorized student learning rights.
9. A college cannot purchase one copy and distribute it to unlimited students.
10. LMS use requires appropriate institutional permission and controlled access.
11. Multi-campus use must be expressly licensed.
12. Enterprise licensing governs internal organizational learning and reference.
13. Commercial Training licensing applies when protected handbook content is commercially used or distributed in paid training.
14. Teaching learned concepts in independently created material is distinct from distributing protected handbook content.
15. Library access and classroom adoption are separate licensing use cases.
16. Government scope must identify the authorized public entity or entities.
17. Complete translation and publication require explicit translation rights.
18. License duration may be ongoing or time-limited depending on the applicable license agreement.
19. License upgrades and conversions should be documented.
20. New special licenses may be created, but their rights and restrictions must be explicit.

---

## Part IV — Copyright Ownership, Contributors & Third-Party Content

**Author and Publisher:** Sharique Chaudhary

**Status:** Master Manual — Part IV

**Relationship:** Continues Part III — License Types & Rights

---

### 1. Purpose and Scope

#### Purpose

Every Engineering Handbook may identify a single author while still involving technical reviewers, editors, diagram designers, translators, developers, researchers, contractors, or other contributors.

The publishing system must accurately identify:

- who created each protected contribution;
- who owns or is authorized to use that contribution;
- the legal basis for those rights; and
- the evidence supporting that conclusion.

A consistent rights-management process protects both the publisher and legitimate contributors while reducing ownership disputes during future editions, translations, remastered releases, and commercial distribution.

#### Scope

This Part establishes the internal publishing standard for:

- copyright ownership;
- authorship;
- joint authorship risk;
- employee and contractor contributions;
- editors and technical reviewers;
- translators;
- illustrators and diagram creators;
- code examples;
- research sources;
- quotations;
- screenshots;
- trademarks;
- open-source material;
- Creative Commons material;
- public-domain material;
- AI-assisted production;
- permissions;
- attribution;
- contributor records; and
- infringement claims.

#### Core Principle

> **DO NOT ASSUME THAT ACCESS TO CONTENT MEANS OWNERSHIP OF CONTENT.**

Finding an image, code sample, diagram, paragraph, research paper, dataset, or other material online does **not** automatically grant permission to copy, modify, reproduce, distribute, or republish that material in a commercial handbook.

Every externally created work must have an identified rights basis before publication.

---

### 2. Copyright Ownership and Authorship Are Related but Distinct

Although closely related, authorship and copyright ownership are separate legal concepts.

**Authorship** identifies the creator of original expression.

**Copyright ownership** identifies the person or entity that owns the relevant exclusive rights under applicable law.

In many situations, the author initially owns copyright.

However, ownership may later be affected by:

- employment law;
- written assignment;
- joint authorship;
- commissioned-work rules;
- inheritance;
- corporate restructuring; or
- the applicable jurisdiction.

Accordingly, the publishing registry must **not** automatically assume:

> **CREATOR = CURRENT COPYRIGHT OWNER**

in every circumstance.

#### Example

A freelance illustrator creates an original storage-engine diagram.

Possible legal arrangements include:

1. The illustrator retains copyright and grants the publisher a publishing license.
2. The illustrator assigns copyright to the publisher.
3. A jurisdiction-specific commissioned-work rule applies.

The correct legal position depends on the applicable agreement and governing law.

For every significant commercial contribution, the rights position should be documented in writing before publication whenever practical.

---

### 3. Sharique Chaudhary's Core Handbook Content

Where Sharique Chaudhary independently creates original handbook text, curriculum expression, diagrams, examples, illustrations, explanations, or other protectable expression, the publication records should identify him as the author and relevant rights holder unless those rights are later validly transferred.

#### Recommended Copyright Notice

```text
Copyright © [Year] Sharique Chaudhary

All rights reserved, except as expressly provided by the applicable license terms or otherwise required by law.
```

The final legal wording should be reviewed before commercial publication.

#### Copyright Notice Limitations

A copyright notice should **not** claim ownership of:

- general engineering facts;
- mathematical truths;
- programming language syntax as such;
- ideas, concepts, methods, systems, or processes beyond the protection available under applicable law;
- third-party copyrighted works;
- third-party trademarks; or
- public-domain material.

A broad copyright notice protects the publication's original protected expression.

It should **not** be interpreted as ownership of an entire engineering discipline, programming language, technology, or field of knowledge.

---

### 4. Copyright Notice Standard

Every formal handbook volume should contain an appropriate copyright notice.

The notice should remain consistent throughout the Engineering Handbook Series.

#### Recommended Fields

A copyright notice should include:

- copyright symbol or equivalent notice;
- publication year;
- copyright owner;
- rights statement;
- edition;
- third-party notice reference where required; and
- licensing statement.

#### Illustrative Notice

```text
Copyright © 2027 Sharique Chaudhary.

All rights reserved.

This publication is licensed, not transferred for unrestricted redistribution.

Use is subject to the applicable handbook license terms.

Third-party names, trademarks, code, quotations, screenshots, diagrams, and other materials remain subject to their respective rights and licenses where identified.
```

This example represents publishing policy and should not be interpreted as jurisdiction-specific legal advice.

#### Copyright Year

The copyright year should accurately reflect the publication history.

Later revisions should not casually replace or erase the original publication year.

Example:

```text
Copyright © 2027 Sharique Chaudhary
```

A materially revised later edition may instead use:

```text
Copyright © 2027, 2030 Sharique Chaudhary
```

or another legally and editorially appropriate formulation.

The final approach should remain consistent across every handbook in the Engineering Handbook Series.

---

### 5. Copyright Registration

Copyright protection and copyright registration are **not** identical concepts.

In many legal systems, copyright protection may arise automatically when an eligible original work is created and fixed in a protectable form.

Registration may nevertheless provide important procedural, evidentiary, or enforcement advantages depending on the applicable jurisdiction.

Because the publisher is based in India, the current Indian copyright registration process and its legal effect should be verified before filing.

#### Publication Records

The publisher should maintain comprehensive publication records, including:

- dated drafts;
- source files;
- version history;
- publication records;
- contributor agreements;
- ISBN records; and
- release records.

These records help establish:

- authorship;
- publication history;
- rights provenance; and
- ownership evidence.

#### Distinguishing Different Systems

Copyright registration should never be confused with other publishing identifiers.

| System | Purpose |
|---------|---------|
| **ISBN** | Publication identification |
| **Copyright Registration** | Rights-related legal registration |
| **License ID** | Customer or organization licensing identity |

Each system serves a different purpose and should be managed independently.

Publication identification does **not** establish copyright ownership.

Likewise, copyright registration does **not** replace ISBN management or customer licensing records.

Together, these systems form separate but complementary parts of the Engineering Handbook publishing infrastructure.

---

### 6. Sole Authorship

A handbook may identify **Sharique Chaudhary** as the sole author when the protectable authorship is genuinely his and other participants perform roles that do **not** create joint authorship under applicable law.

Examples of supporting roles may include:

- proofreading;
- factual checking;
- technical review;
- formatting; and
- administrative assistance.

Role titles alone do **not** determine legal authorship.

A person identified as a **Technical Reviewer** may contribute substantial original expression.

Likewise, a person identified as a **Co-Author** may, in reality, contribute very little protectable expression.

The actual contribution and the applicable law determine authorship—not the title assigned to an individual.

Contributor agreements should accurately describe the work being performed and the intended legal relationship before publication whenever practical.

---

### 7. Joint Authorship Risk

Joint authorship may arise when multiple individuals contribute protectable original expression to a combined work under the legal requirements of the applicable jurisdiction.

This can create significant ownership and licensing consequences.

#### Example

Two engineers collaboratively write multiple chapters, repeatedly revise each other's work, and intend to create one unified handbook.

Merely describing one individual as a **Technical Reviewer** does not automatically eliminate potential joint-authorship questions.

Before accepting substantial original writing from another contributor, the intended relationship should be clearly established.

Possible arrangements include:

- co-authorship;
- commissioned contribution;
- licensed contribution; or
- assigned contribution.

Whenever practical, the chosen arrangement should be documented before publication.

The publisher should never rely on informal assumptions such as:

> **"We are friends, so the rights are obvious."**

Commercial publishing requires clear documentation rather than verbal understandings.

---

### 8. Contributor Classification

Every contributor should be classified according to the role actually performed.

Recommended contributor classifications include:

- Author
- Co-Author
- Contributing Author
- Technical Reviewer
- Editor
- Copy Editor
- Proofreader
- Illustrator
- Diagram Designer
- Translator
- Code Reviewer
- Research Assistant
- Accessibility Reviewer
- Security Reviewer
- Contractor

A contributor may perform multiple roles within the same publication.

#### Example

```text
Contributor:
Aisha Example

Roles:
• Technical Reviewer
• Code Reviewer
```

Publication credits should accurately reflect meaningful contributions.

Contributor classification assists publication management but does **not** determine ownership of copyright.

The applicable contributor agreement remains the authoritative rights document.

---

### 9. Contributor Agreements

Significant contributors should execute written contributor agreements.

A contributor agreement may define:

- contributor identity;
- project;
- handbook;
- edition or volume scope;
- role;
- expected contribution;
- compensation;
- deadlines;
- confidentiality;
- ownership;
- assignment or license;
- attribution;
- moral-rights treatment where legally applicable;
- warranties;
- third-party material restrictions;
- revision rights;
- termination; and
- dispute-resolution process.

The agreement should be signed or accepted through an appropriate legally recognized process.

The publisher should preserve:

- the executed agreement;
- the agreement version; and
- evidence of acceptance.

Contributor agreement templates copied from the internet should **not** be adopted without appropriate legal review.

The final agreement should reflect the governing jurisdiction and the actual publishing model of the Engineering Handbook Series.

---

### 10. Copyright Assignment vs License

Two common rights structures are **Assignment** and **License**.

Although both authorize publication under defined circumstances, they produce very different legal results.

#### Assignment

An assignment transfers specified copyright ownership rights, subject to applicable law and the written agreement.

#### License

A license authorizes specified uses while copyright ownership may remain with the original rights holder.

#### Example — Licensed Contribution

An illustrator retains copyright but grants the publisher an exclusive worldwide license to reproduce the illustration in the handbook and future authorized editions.

#### Example — Assigned Contribution

The illustrator instead assigns the specified copyright rights to the publisher.

The publisher should deliberately choose the appropriate rights model rather than relying on assumptions.

The publishing workflow should avoid vague statements such as:

> **"You made it for my book, so now everything belongs to me."**

The scope of rights should always be documented.

Questions that should be addressed include:

- Is the license exclusive or non-exclusive?
- What territory is covered?
- What is the duration?
- Which publication formats are included?
- Does the agreement include translations?
- Does it apply to future editions?
- Does it permit marketing use?
- Are adaptation rights included?
- Is sublicensing permitted?
- Under what conditions may the agreement terminate?

Clear documentation at the beginning of a project is significantly easier than resolving ownership disputes after publication.

---

### 11. Decision Summary

This Part establishes the ownership and rights-management framework for the Engineering Handbook Series. It defines who owns original content, how contributors should be managed, how third-party material should be handled, and how copyright responsibilities should be documented throughout the publication lifecycle.

#### 11.1 Summary

1. Copyright ownership and authorship are related but legally distinct concepts.
2. Access to content does not automatically create ownership or republication rights.
3. Original handbook content independently created by Sharique Chaudhary should identify him as the author and rights holder unless those rights are later validly transferred.
4. Copyright notices should protect original expression without claiming ownership over facts, ideas, public-domain material, or third-party rights.
5. Every handbook should include a consistent copyright notice and publication history.
6. Copyright registration, ISBN, and License ID are separate publishing systems and should never be treated as interchangeable.
7. Sole authorship should reflect the actual creation of protectable expression rather than contributor titles.
8. Joint authorship risks should be evaluated before accepting substantial external contributions.
9. Contributor roles should accurately reflect the work performed but do not themselves determine ownership.
10. Significant contributors should execute written contributor agreements before substantial work begins whenever practical.
11. Contributor agreements should clearly define ownership, licenses, attribution, responsibilities, confidentiality, revisions, and dispute procedures.
12. Assignment transfers specified copyright ownership, whereas a license grants defined usage rights without necessarily transferring ownership.
13. Rights arrangements should always be documented rather than assumed.
14. Every significant commercial contribution should have a documented legal basis before publication.
15. Rights documentation should become a standard part of the Engineering Handbook publishing workflow.

---

## Part V — Digital Rights, License Identity & Watermarking

**Author and Publisher:** Sharique Chaudhary

**Status:** Master Manual — Part V

**Relationship:** Continues Part IV — Copyright Ownership, Contributors & Third-Party Content

---

### 1. Purpose of Digital Rights Management

#### Purpose

Digital publishing introduces a fundamental operational challenge: a digital handbook can be copied perfectly, distributed rapidly, and reproduced at almost no cost.

The objective of the Engineering Handbook digital-rights system is **not** to make copying mathematically impossible.

No PDF protection mechanism, Digital Rights Management (DRM) technology, watermark, encryption method, or license server can guarantee that a determined attacker will never duplicate or redistribute handbook content.

Instead, the objective is to establish a licensing ecosystem that provides:

- clear license identity;
- controlled authorized distribution;
- traceable entitlement records;
- licensed-copy identification;
- reasonable deterrence against unauthorized redistribution;
- leak investigation capability;
- evidence preservation; and
- scalable license verification.

#### Core Principle

The digital-rights system exists to **support licensing**, not to inconvenience legitimate license holders.

Digital-rights mechanisms should therefore balance:

- security;
- usability;
- privacy; and
- operational practicality.

#### Relationship to Licensing

Digital-rights controls are supporting mechanisms for the licensing framework.

They **do not replace**:

- the License Agreement;
- copyright ownership;
- customer licensing records;
- entitlement management; or
- enforcement procedures.

Copyright ownership remains with the publisher unless explicitly transferred through a separate legal agreement.

Customers receive a **license to use** the handbook under defined terms. They do **not** acquire ownership of the handbook or its intellectual property.

---

### 2. Digital Identity Model

The Engineering Handbook ecosystem should use multiple structured identities rather than a single universal identifier.

Each identity represents a different business entity within the publishing system.

Keeping these identities independent improves:

- long-term maintainability;
- database normalization;
- licensing accuracy;
- publication management;
- auditability; and
- future scalability.

#### Core Identity Types

| Identity | Purpose |
|----------|---------|
| **Handbook ID** | Identifies an independent handbook family. |
| **Volume ID** | Identifies a specific volume within a handbook. |
| **Edition ID** | Identifies a formally published edition. |
| **License ID** | Identifies a licensing relationship between the publisher and a license holder. |
| **Entitlement ID** | Identifies a granted usage entitlement within a license. |
| **Customer / Organization ID** | Identifies the licensed individual or organization. |

These identifiers solve different operational problems and should never be collapsed into a single universal identifier.

Each identifier has its own lifecycle and should remain independently manageable.

#### Illustrative Example

```text
Customer:        CUS-0001842
License:         LIC-PER-8F2K7M91
Handbook:        HB-DATABASE
Edition:         ED-01
Volume:          VOL-007
Entitlement:     ENT-91KD72
```

The exact production format may evolve over time.

The conceptual separation between identity types should remain stable across future implementations.

---

### 3. Handbook ID

A **Handbook ID** uniquely identifies an independent handbook family.

It identifies the publication itself—not a particular edition, volume, revision, license, customer, or generated PDF.

#### Illustrative Examples

```text
HB-OS
HB-LINUX
HB-NETWORKS
HB-DATABASE
HB-BACKEND
HB-SECURITY
HB-AI
```

#### Design Requirements

A Handbook ID should be:

- unique;
- stable throughout the handbook's lifetime;
- machine-readable;
- reasonably concise; and
- independent of the handbook's public marketing title whenever practical.

Public branding may evolve without affecting licensing records or database relationships.

#### Example

A handbook initially published as:

```text
AI Engineering Handbook
```

may later be renamed:

```text
AI Systems Engineering Handbook
```

If both publications belong to the same handbook family, the Handbook ID may continue to remain:

```text
HB-AI
```

A Handbook ID should never be reassigned to an unrelated publication after retirement.

Historical identity should always remain preserved.

---

### 4. Volume ID

A **Volume ID** identifies a specific publishing volume within a handbook family.

Unlike display numbering, the Volume ID provides a permanent publication identity for licensing and historical recordkeeping.

#### Illustrative Format

```text
HB-DATABASE-VOL-001
HB-DATABASE-VOL-002
HB-DATABASE-VOL-003
```

The publishing platform may internally use shorter identifiers while presenting more reader-friendly publication codes externally.

#### Volume Number vs Volume ID

A displayed volume number is primarily editorial.

A Volume ID is an internal publication identity.

Although related, they should not be considered interchangeable.

Future editions may:

- merge volumes;
- split volumes;
- renumber volumes; or
- reorganize content.

Historical licensing records must continue identifying the originally licensed publication.

The licensing database should therefore never rely solely upon labels such as:

> **Volume 4**

#### Merge Example

**Old Edition**

```text
Volume 4
Volume 5
```

**New Edition**

```text
Consolidated Volume 4
```

Even after consolidation:

- original Volume IDs should remain preserved;
- historical license records should remain valid; and
- the consolidated publication should receive its own publication identity.

Licensing history should always remain traceable across editorial restructuring.

---

### 5. Edition ID

An **Edition ID** identifies a formally published edition of a handbook.

An Edition represents a significant publishing milestone rather than ordinary corrections or maintenance updates.

#### Illustrative Examples

```text
ED-01
ED-02
ED-RM01
```

or

```text
HB-DATABASE-ED-01
HB-DATABASE-ED-RM01
```

#### Edition Types

Edition IDs may distinguish:

- First Edition;
- Second Edition;
- Remastered Edition; and
- other formally recognized editions.

Minor corrections should normally use revision metadata rather than creating a new Edition ID.

#### Edition vs Revision

These concepts should remain distinct.

| Concept | Purpose |
|---------|---------|
| **Edition** | Major officially published release. |
| **Revision** | Corrections, improvements, or maintenance within an edition. |
| **Reprint** | Additional production of the same edition without creating a new edition. |

#### Example

```text
Edition:
ED-01

Revision:
1.0.3
```

The license holder remains licensed for the **First Edition** while receiving an updated revision of that same edition where permitted under the applicable license.

The licensing system should permanently associate every entitlement with its corresponding Edition ID to preserve publication history, licensing accuracy, and future upgrade paths.

---

## Part VI — Anti-Piracy & Enforcement Strategy

---

### 1. Purpose of the Anti-Piracy Strategy

The Engineering Handbook ecosystem requires a comprehensive anti-piracy strategy because digital publications can be copied, uploaded, mirrored, redistributed, resold, reproduced, and distributed worldwide within minutes. Although piracy cannot be eliminated entirely through technical, contractual, or legal measures alone, a structured anti-piracy framework can significantly reduce unauthorized distribution, strengthen accountability, improve investigative accuracy, and protect the integrity of the licensing ecosystem.

The purpose of this Anti-Piracy Strategy is not to claim that every unauthorized act can be prevented. Rather, it establishes a practical and legally defensible framework for preventing avoidable misuse, detecting unauthorized distribution, preserving reliable evidence, conducting fair investigations, and applying proportionate enforcement measures where appropriate.

The objectives of this Anti-Piracy Strategy are to:

- reduce casual and unintentional unauthorized sharing;
- deter deliberate redistribution of licensed handbook content;
- identify the source of leaked licensed copies where reasonably possible;
- detect organized, systematic, and commercial piracy activities;
- preserve reliable evidence for technical, contractual, and legal investigations;
- facilitate the lawful removal of unauthorized copies through appropriate channels;
- enforce licensing terms fairly, consistently, and proportionately;
- protect legitimate license holders from unsupported or incorrect allegations; and
- maintain a documented, repeatable, and auditable incident-response process.

This strategy recognizes that not every licensing issue constitutes piracy. Ordinary educational use, genuine administrative mistakes, technical failures, account-security incidents, and deliberate copyright infringement are fundamentally different situations that require different investigative approaches and different enforcement responses. Accordingly, each incident shall be assessed according to its specific facts, available evidence, scale, intent, and surrounding circumstances rather than through automatic assumptions or predetermined conclusions.

An effective anti-piracy program is not measured by the number of warnings issued, licenses suspended, or enforcement actions taken. Instead, its effectiveness is measured by its ability to:

- reach accurate, objective, and evidence-based conclusions;
- preserve the integrity and reliability of investigative evidence;
- distinguish between accidental mistakes and deliberate misconduct;
- apply responses that are proportionate to the seriousness of each incident;
- protect legitimate readers and license holders from false accusations; and
- produce enforcement decisions capable of withstanding contractual, operational, and legal scrutiny.

Accordingly, every anti-piracy measure established throughout this Part shall be interpreted in a manner that balances the legitimate protection of intellectual property with fairness, due process, and respect for the rights of licensed customers. The objective of enforcement is to preserve the integrity of the licensing system—not to maximize punishment or create unnecessary barriers for legitimate educational use.

### 2. Definition of Piracy Within the Handbook Ecosystem

For the purposes of this Manual, **piracy** generally refers to the unauthorized reproduction, distribution, sale, public availability, or systematic provision of protected handbook content beyond the rights expressly granted under the applicable license.

Examples of piracy may include:

- uploading complete handbook files to piracy websites;
- distributing handbook files through publicly accessible cloud storage or file-sharing services;
- sharing complete handbook copies within large Telegram, Discord, WhatsApp, or similar communities beyond the licensed scope;
- distributing handbook publications through torrent networks or peer-to-peer services;
- selling unauthorized digital copies of handbook publications;
- printing and commercially distributing unauthorized physical reproductions;
- operating websites or services that provide unauthorized downloads of handbook publications;
- using a single licensed copy as a source for unlimited organizational or public distribution; and
- intentionally removing, modifying, or concealing license-identification or watermarking information before redistribution.

Piracy should not be presumed merely because copyrighted content appears outside the publisher's distribution systems. Not every copyright dispute, licensing issue, or unauthorized use automatically constitutes piracy.

Examples requiring independent legal and factual assessment include:

- a limited quotation used within academic or research work;
- a screenshot reproduced for criticism, commentary, review, or similar lawful purposes;
- an accidental Learning Management System (LMS) configuration error;
- unauthorized access resulting from a compromised customer account;
- continued employee access caused by an organizational offboarding failure;
- technical processing or document conversion that unintentionally removes metadata or document properties; and
- other circumstances where the available evidence does not reasonably establish intentional unauthorized redistribution.

Accordingly, the existence of an unauthorized copy alone should not determine the outcome of an investigation. Every incident should be evaluated according to the available evidence, the scale of the distribution, the surrounding circumstances, the apparent intent of the parties involved, and the applicable contractual and legal framework before any enforcement action is considered.

The objective of this definition is to establish a consistent operational standard for identifying potential piracy while ensuring that legitimate educational use, good-faith mistakes, security incidents, and other non-piratical circumstances are distinguished from deliberate copyright infringement.

### 3. Threat Model

An effective anti-piracy strategy begins with a clear understanding of the different forms of unauthorized activity that may threaten the Engineering Handbook ecosystem. Not all threats present the same level of risk, require the same evidence, or justify the same response. Accordingly, the publisher shall classify suspected incidents according to the nature of the activity before determining the appropriate investigative or enforcement process.

The following threat categories provide the operational model for identifying and assessing potential piracy incidents.

#### 3.1 Casual Sharing

Casual sharing occurs when a licensed customer provides handbook content to one or more individuals beyond the scope of the applicable license without operating a broader redistribution scheme.

**Example**

A license holder sends a complete handbook volume to a friend or a small private study group despite holding only an individual license.

Although the scale of distribution may be limited, repeated or intentional sharing may still constitute a material license violation depending on the circumstances.

---

#### 3.2 Mass Redistribution

Mass redistribution involves making handbook publications available to a substantially larger audience through public or semi-public distribution channels.

Examples include:

- uploading handbook files to public cloud storage;
- publishing downloadable copies on websites or forums;
- distributing complete handbook collections through large messaging communities;
- providing unrestricted access through public file-sharing services; or
- distributing handbook publications through peer-to-peer or torrent networks.

Mass redistribution significantly increases the potential harm to the publishing ecosystem and therefore generally requires a higher enforcement priority.

---

#### 3.3 Commercial Piracy

Commercial piracy occurs when an individual or organization derives, or attempts to derive, financial or commercial benefit from the unauthorized reproduction or distribution of handbook publications.

Examples include:

- selling unauthorized digital handbook copies;
- charging for access to pirated handbook collections;
- operating commercial piracy websites;
- selling counterfeit printed editions; or
- offering subscription-based access to unauthorized copies.

Commercial piracy represents one of the highest-risk categories because it intentionally exploits protected intellectual property for financial gain.

---

#### 3.4 Institutional Under-Licensing

Institutional under-licensing occurs when an organization knowingly distributes handbook content beyond the number of licenses or authorized users covered by its agreement.

**Example**

An institution purchases licenses for a limited number of students or employees but intentionally provides access to a substantially larger population.

Ordinary administrative discrepancies should be distinguished from deliberate under-licensing through appropriate investigation and license reconciliation procedures.

---

#### 3.5 Account Sharing

Account sharing occurs when a licensed account is intentionally used by multiple unrelated individuals in a manner inconsistent with the applicable license terms.

Examples may include:

- sharing login credentials among unrelated users;
- maintaining communal accounts for multiple individuals; or
- intentionally allowing repeated access by persons who are not authorized under the license.

This category should be distinguished from legitimate multi-device access expressly permitted under the applicable license.

---

#### 3.6 Credential Compromise

Credential compromise occurs when a legitimate account is accessed by an unauthorized party due to theft, phishing, malware, password disclosure, or another security incident.

A compromised account should not automatically be treated as evidence of intentional piracy. Instead, investigators should assess available security records before reaching any conclusion regarding responsibility.

---

#### 3.7 Watermark Removal

Watermark removal involves the deliberate removal, alteration, concealment, or degradation of visible or embedded license-identification information intended to prevent traceability.

Examples include:

- removing purchaser identification from document pages;
- cropping licensed watermark regions;
- modifying document metadata associated with license identity; or
- attempting to conceal document-identification information before redistribution.

The removal of watermark information may constitute important investigative evidence but does not, by itself, establish the identity of the person responsible for unauthorized distribution.

---

#### 3.8 Content Cloning

Content cloning occurs when a third party reproduces substantial portions of the original protected expression contained within the handbook series and presents that material as a competing or substitute publication.

Examples may include the substantial reproduction of:

- original explanations;
- diagrams;
- curriculum structure;
- illustrations;
- exercises;
- examples; or
- other protected original expression.

General subject matter, technical concepts, and educational topics remain outside the scope of exclusive ownership. Investigations should therefore distinguish protected expression from unprotectable ideas or facts.

---

#### 3.9 Counterfeit Editions

Counterfeit editions are unauthorized publications that falsely represent themselves as official, authorized, updated, translated, or publisher-approved editions of the Engineering Handbook Series.

Such publications may mislead readers regarding authenticity while simultaneously infringing copyright, trademark, or other applicable intellectual property rights.

---

#### 3.10 Insider Leaks

Insider leaks occur when an individual with legitimate access to protected handbook materials—including collaborators, contractors, institutional administrators, reviewers, translators, designers, developers, or other authorized parties—discloses or distributes those materials without authorization.

Because insiders may possess access to unpublished content, source files, or other privileged publishing assets, insider leaks may present risks significantly greater than ordinary customer redistribution.

---
Each threat category described in this Section requires its own investigative methodology, evidentiary requirements, operational procedures, and proportionate response. Consequently, no single enforcement model should be applied uniformly to every suspected piracy incident.

### 4. Layered Anti-Piracy Model

No single technical, contractual, or legal control is sufficient to protect digital publications against unauthorized distribution. Effective anti-piracy protection requires multiple complementary safeguards that work together throughout the publication lifecycle. The failure of one control should not automatically result in the failure of the entire protection framework.

Accordingly, the Engineering Handbook Series adopts a **layered anti-piracy model**, in which each layer addresses a different aspect of prevention, traceability, investigation, or enforcement.

#### 4.1 Layer 1 — Clear Licensing Terms

The first layer of protection is a licensing model that is easy to understand and capable of being applied consistently.

Readers, institutions, and organizations should be able to determine:

- what their license permits;
- what activities require additional authorization;
- what restrictions apply to redistribution and sharing; and
- what consequences may follow a material license violation.

Clear licensing reduces accidental misuse and supports fair enforcement.

---

#### 4.2 Layer 2 — License Identity

Every licensed publication should be capable of being linked to an identifiable licensing record.

This may include:

- License IDs;
- entitlement records;
- edition information;
- document instance identifiers; and
- other operational identifiers used to distinguish authorized distributions.

License identity establishes the foundation for traceability and subsequent investigations.

---

#### 4.3 Layer 3 — Personalized Watermarking

Where technically and operationally appropriate, licensed digital publications should contain personalized watermarking or other license-identification information associated with the licensed customer or organization.

Personalized watermarking primarily serves three purposes:

- discouraging unauthorized redistribution;
- supporting investigative traceability; and
- strengthening evidentiary records during incident investigations.

Watermarking is an investigative tool and should not be interpreted as automatic proof of responsibility.

---

#### 4.4 Layer 4 — Controlled Distribution

Licensed publications should be delivered through authorized distribution systems rather than through publicly exposed master-file locations.

Appropriate delivery controls may include:

- authenticated downloads;
- controlled access portals;
- entitlement verification;
- secure delivery mechanisms; and
- protection of master publication files.

Reducing unnecessary exposure of master files lowers the overall risk of unauthorized redistribution.

---

#### 4.5 Layer 5 — Audit and Security Records

Significant licensing, entitlement, and publication-delivery events should be recorded where operationally appropriate.

Examples include:

- document delivery;
- entitlement changes;
- license modifications;
- security-related events; and
- other records relevant to future investigations.

Audit records should be proportionate to operational needs and managed in accordance with applicable legal and privacy obligations.

---

#### 4.6 Layer 6 — Discovery

The publisher should maintain practical methods for identifying significant unauthorized distribution.

Discovery activities may include:

- reviewing piracy reports;
- monitoring publicly available sources;
- investigating marketplace listings;
- analyzing watermark information; and
- reviewing other credible reports of suspected infringement.

Discovery initiates an investigation; it does not establish liability.

---

#### 4.7 Layer 7 — Incident Investigation

Before any significant enforcement action is taken, available evidence should be collected, preserved, verified, and evaluated.

An effective investigation should seek to determine:

- what occurred;
- how the incident occurred;
- the scale of the distribution;
- the applicable license;
- the available technical evidence; and
- whether alternative explanations reasonably exist.

Enforcement decisions should be based upon documented findings rather than assumptions.

---

#### 4.8 Layer 8 — Proportionate Response

Responses should always reflect the seriousness of the incident rather than applying identical consequences to every violation.

Relevant considerations include:

- the scale of unauthorized distribution;
- apparent intent;
- repetition;
- commercial benefit;
- cooperation during the investigation;
- evidence of account compromise;
- corrective actions taken; and
- the overall impact upon the licensing ecosystem.

Proportionate enforcement strengthens both fairness and long-term credibility.

---
No individual layer provides complete protection against piracy.

A password-protected document without licensing records provides limited investigative value.

A legal notice without technical traceability may be difficult to enforce in practice.

Similarly, personalized watermarking without documented investigative procedures provides identification but not an effective enforcement framework.

The anti-piracy strategy should therefore be implemented as an integrated system in which technical safeguards, licensing controls, operational processes, and legal enforcement reinforce one another rather than operating independently.

### 5. Prevention Before Enforcement

The primary objective of an effective anti-piracy strategy should be the prevention of avoidable misuse rather than the pursuit of enforcement after a violation has occurred. Preventive measures reduce the likelihood of infringement, improve user understanding, and strengthen compliance while minimizing unnecessary disputes between the publisher and legitimate license holders.

Accordingly, the Engineering Handbook licensing ecosystem should prioritize practical preventive controls before relying upon investigative or enforcement measures.

Preventive measures may include:

- clear and easily understandable license summaries on product and purchase pages;
- visible license-holder watermarking within licensed publications;
- unique License IDs and Document Instance IDs;
- controlled and authenticated download mechanisms;
- reasonable account-security measures;
- institutional seat-management controls;
- straightforward license-upgrade and expansion procedures;
- affordable educational pricing where commercially sustainable;
- accessible support for legitimate device replacement and account recovery; and
- clearly documented rules governing editions, bundles, and license transfers where applicable.

A licensing system that is difficult to understand increases the likelihood of accidental non-compliance.

For example, if an educational institution cannot reasonably determine whether uploading a handbook to its Learning Management System (LMS) is permitted under its license, the licensing documentation has failed to communicate an important operational rule. In such circumstances, the publisher should improve the clarity of the licensing framework rather than relying upon ambiguity to support future enforcement.

This principle does **not** authorize or excuse unauthorized distribution. Rather, it recognizes that clear communication is itself an important component of copyright protection and license compliance.

The anti-piracy framework should therefore seek to eliminate unnecessary confusion before addressing intentional misconduct. Legitimate readers should be able to understand their rights and obligations without requiring legal interpretation or individualized clarification for routine use.

Wherever reasonably possible, friction should be concentrated around activities that present a genuine risk of unauthorized redistribution rather than ordinary reading, legitimate study, or other authorized use. Effective anti-piracy systems should discourage infringement while preserving a straightforward and predictable experience for compliant license holders.

Accordingly, prevention, education, and clear licensing should always form the first line of defense, with investigative and enforcement measures serving as proportionate responses when preventive controls have failed or have been deliberately circumvented.

### 6. Customer Education and Awareness

An informed license holder is significantly less likely to violate licensing terms than one who is uncertain about the scope of permitted use. Accordingly, customer education should form an integral component of the Engineering Handbook anti-piracy strategy.

The publisher should make reasonable efforts to communicate licensing rights and restrictions through clear, accessible, and consistent documentation rather than relying solely on legal language contained within lengthy agreements.

Educational measures may include:

- concise license summaries accompanying handbook purchases;
- frequently asked questions (FAQs);
- examples illustrating permitted and prohibited uses;
- guidance for educational institutions and organizations;
- onboarding information for new license holders; and
- support channels capable of addressing licensing questions before a violation occurs.

Customer education is intended to reduce unintentional misuse and promote voluntary compliance. It should not be interpreted as limiting the publisher's ability to investigate or enforce material license violations where appropriate.

---

### 7. Security Responsibilities of License Holders

Every license holder shares responsibility for protecting licensed handbook publications from unauthorized access and distribution.

Reasonable security practices may include:

- maintaining the confidentiality of account credentials;
- using strong and unique passwords;
- enabling multi-factor authentication where available;
- protecting downloaded handbook files from unauthorized access;
- avoiding distribution through unsecured public storage services;
- reporting suspected credential compromise promptly; and
- complying with any applicable organizational information-security policies.

License holders are expected to exercise reasonable care under the circumstances. However, the occurrence of a cybersecurity incident, malware infection, phishing attack, or other security compromise shall not automatically constitute evidence of intentional piracy.

Where a license holder promptly reports a security incident and cooperates in the resulting investigation, such cooperation may be considered when determining the appropriate response.

The publisher likewise retains responsibility for implementing reasonable administrative, technical, and operational safeguards appropriate to its publishing infrastructure. Effective anti-piracy protection is therefore a shared responsibility between the publisher and authorized license holders.

### 8. Reporting Suspected Piracy

The publisher encourages responsible reporting of suspected piracy affecting the Engineering Handbook Series. Timely reporting assists in protecting the integrity of the licensing ecosystem while reducing the long-term impact of unauthorized distribution.

Reports may originate from:

- licensed customers;
- educational institutions;
- organizational administrators;
- contributors;
- publishing partners;
- members of the public; or
- internal monitoring activities.

A report should, where reasonably possible, include:

- the location of the suspected unauthorized content;
- copies of relevant webpages or screenshots;
- download links or file identifiers;
- the date and time the material was observed;
- any available license-identification information; and
- any other information that may assist the investigation.

Submitting a report does not establish that piracy has occurred. Every report should undergo an appropriate verification process before investigative or enforcement decisions are made.

The publisher should also take reasonable steps to protect the confidentiality of individuals reporting suspected infringement where appropriate and permitted by law.

---

### 9. Initial Incident Assessment

Upon receiving information suggesting a potential license violation or piracy incident, the publisher should conduct an initial assessment before initiating formal enforcement procedures.

The purpose of the assessment is to determine whether sufficient information exists to justify further investigation.

An initial assessment may consider:

- the credibility of the report;
- whether protected handbook content is involved;
- the apparent scale of the distribution;
- whether the reported activity appears intentional or accidental;
- the existence of relevant licensing records;
- available technical evidence; and
- whether immediate preservation of evidence is necessary.

Where the available information is insufficient or unreliable, additional evidence should be obtained before reaching conclusions regarding responsibility.

Not every reported incident requires a full investigation. Minor administrative issues, obvious reporting errors, duplicate reports, or incidents lacking credible supporting evidence may be closed following appropriate documentation.

---

### 10. Evidence Preservation

Evidence collected during piracy investigations should be preserved in a manner that supports accuracy, consistency, and future verification.

Reasonable evidence preservation practices may include:

- capturing webpages before they are removed or modified;
- preserving download links and associated metadata;
- recording dates and times of observations;
- retaining relevant screenshots;
- documenting investigative actions;
- preserving watermark or license-identification information; and
- maintaining internal investigation records.

Evidence should be collected using methods that preserve its integrity as far as reasonably practicable. Original records should not be unnecessarily altered, modified, or overwritten during the investigation process.

Where technical evidence is obtained from automated systems, reasonable efforts should be made to record the source of the information and any relevant contextual details necessary for later review.

Evidence preservation supports informed decision-making and helps ensure that subsequent enforcement actions are based upon documented facts rather than recollection, assumption, or incomplete information.

### 11. Investigation Procedures

Where an initial assessment indicates that further action is warranted, the publisher should conduct an investigation using procedures that are fair, documented, and proportionate to the nature of the suspected incident.

The objectives of an investigation include determining:

- whether unauthorized distribution has occurred;
- the scope and duration of the activity;
- the applicable license rights;
- the available technical and documentary evidence;
- whether alternative explanations reasonably exist; and
- the appropriate operational or legal response.

Investigations should rely upon verifiable evidence rather than assumption or speculation. All material findings should be documented to the extent reasonably practicable.

Where additional information becomes available during the investigation, previous conclusions should be reviewed and updated accordingly.

Investigative activities should remain proportionate to the seriousness of the incident and should avoid unnecessary intrusion into the legitimate rights of license holders.

---

### 12. Cooperation During Investigations

The publisher may request reasonable cooperation from individuals or organizations involved in a piracy investigation where such cooperation is necessary to establish the relevant facts.

Reasonable cooperation may include:

- confirming licensing information;
- providing purchase or entitlement records;
- explaining the circumstances surrounding the reported activity;
- assisting in identifying compromised accounts;
- preserving relevant evidence; and
- responding to legitimate investigative inquiries within a reasonable period.

Cooperation should not be interpreted as an admission of liability, nor should the absence of immediate cooperation automatically be treated as evidence of intentional infringement.

Where a license holder demonstrates good-faith cooperation, promptly addresses the identified issue, and takes reasonable corrective action, such conduct may be considered when determining the appropriate response.

---

### 13. Good-Faith Errors

Not every licensing violation results from intentional misconduct. Some incidents arise from misunderstanding, administrative oversight, technical error, or other good-faith mistakes.

Examples may include:

- misunderstanding the scope of an institutional license;
- accidental distribution within an internal collaboration platform;
- unintended public exposure resulting from cloud-storage configuration errors;
- unauthorized access following credential compromise; or
- other circumstances where the available evidence does not reasonably indicate deliberate infringement.

Where the evidence supports the conclusion that a violation occurred in good faith, the publisher should, where appropriate, prioritize corrective action, education, and future compliance before considering more severe enforcement measures.

Nothing in this Section prevents the publisher from taking appropriate action where continued non-compliance persists after notice or where the resulting harm remains significant despite the absence of malicious intent.

### 14. Corrective Actions

Where an investigation confirms that a license violation has occurred, the publisher should first consider corrective actions that effectively restore compliance before pursuing more severe enforcement measures, provided such an approach is appropriate under the circumstances.

Corrective actions may include:

- removing unauthorized copies from public or shared locations;
- discontinuing unauthorized distribution;
- correcting licensing deficiencies;
- upgrading or expanding an existing license;
- replacing compromised credentials;
- implementing additional security measures;
- providing institutional compliance assurances; or
- completing other reasonable remedial actions requested by the publisher.

The selection of corrective measures should reflect:

- the seriousness of the violation;
- the scale of the unauthorized distribution;
- the license holder's cooperation;
- any previous compliance history; and
- the likelihood of preventing future incidents.

Successful completion of corrective actions does not automatically eliminate liability for prior violations. However, good-faith remediation may be considered when determining the appropriate operational or legal response.

---

### 15. Escalation Framework

The Engineering Handbook anti-piracy strategy adopts a graduated enforcement model in which responses are proportionate to the nature, severity, and persistence of the violation. Escalation should occur only after the available evidence has been appropriately reviewed.

An illustrative escalation framework may include:

#### Level 1 — Educational Guidance

Suitable where the incident appears to result from misunderstanding, administrative oversight, or another good-faith error.

Typical actions may include:

- providing clarification of license terms;
- requesting voluntary compliance;
- issuing educational guidance; or
- documenting the incident for future reference.

---

#### Level 2 — Formal Compliance Notice

Appropriate where corrective action is required to restore compliance or where an initial educational response has not resolved the issue.

Actions may include:

- issuing a written notice;
- requesting removal of unauthorized copies;
- requiring confirmation of corrective actions; or
- establishing a reasonable compliance deadline.

---

#### Level 3 — License Restrictions

Where material non-compliance continues despite reasonable opportunities for correction, the publisher may impose proportionate administrative restrictions consistent with the applicable license agreement.

Examples include:

- temporary suspension of download privileges;
- restriction of specific licensed services;
- temporary account limitations; or
- suspension pending completion of an investigation.

---

#### Level 4 — License Termination

Where the violation is serious, repeated, fraudulent, commercial in nature, or otherwise constitutes a material breach of the applicable agreement, the publisher may terminate the relevant license in accordance with the governing contractual terms and applicable law.

Termination should occur only after appropriate procedural requirements have been satisfied unless immediate action is justified by exceptional circumstances.

---

#### Level 5 — Legal Enforcement

Where voluntary compliance cannot reasonably be achieved or where significant infringement, commercial piracy, counterfeiting, or other serious misconduct has occurred, the publisher may pursue appropriate legal remedies available under applicable law.

Such remedies may include civil proceedings, injunctive relief, claims for damages, contractual remedies, or other lawful enforcement mechanisms considered appropriate for the particular circumstances.

---
The escalation framework is intended to promote fairness, consistency, and proportionality. It does not require every incident to progress sequentially through each level. Serious violations may justify immediate escalation where supported by the available evidence and applicable legal or contractual rights.

### 16. Notice and Takedown Procedures

Where unauthorized distribution of handbook publications is identified on third-party platforms, websites, marketplaces, or online services, the publisher may request the removal or restriction of access to the infringing material through applicable notice-and-takedown procedures.

A takedown request should, where reasonably practicable, include:

- identification of the copyrighted work;
- identification of the allegedly infringing material;
- sufficient information to enable the service provider to locate the material;
- the legal basis supporting the request;
- appropriate contact information for the publisher or its authorized representative; and
- any additional information required by the applicable platform or governing law.

The publisher should make reasonable efforts to ensure that takedown requests are accurate, supported by available evidence, and submitted in good faith.

Where a platform provides a formal counter-notification or dispute process, the publisher should evaluate any response objectively before pursuing further enforcement measures.

---

### 17. Repeat Infringers

Repeated or persistent violations of licensing terms may warrant enhanced scrutiny and proportionately stronger enforcement measures.

In determining whether an individual or organization should be treated as a repeat infringer, the publisher may consider:

- the number of confirmed violations;
- the time between incidents;
- the seriousness of previous violations;
- prior warnings or compliance notices;
- evidence of intentional misconduct;
- corrective actions previously undertaken; and
- the overall pattern of non-compliance.

Minor administrative errors, isolated good-faith mistakes, or incidents resulting from verified security compromises should not ordinarily establish repeat-infringer status without additional evidence demonstrating continuing non-compliance.

Where repeat infringement is established, the publisher may consider additional administrative, contractual, or legal measures consistent with the applicable license agreement and governing law.

---

### 18. Commercial Piracy Response

Commercial piracy presents a significantly greater threat to the Engineering Handbook ecosystem than isolated unauthorized sharing because it intentionally exploits protected intellectual property for financial or commercial advantage.

Where credible evidence indicates commercial piracy, the publisher may consider more immediate and comprehensive enforcement measures than would ordinarily apply to non-commercial violations.

Such measures may include:

- expedited investigation;
- preservation of additional technical evidence;
- marketplace reporting;
- notice-and-takedown requests;
- suspension or termination of applicable licenses;
- engagement with legal counsel;
- cooperation with payment providers or hosting services where appropriate; and
- pursuit of available contractual, civil, or other lawful remedies.

Commercial piracy investigations should nevertheless remain evidence-based and proportionate. Allegations of commercial infringement should not be made without reasonable factual support.

The objective of these measures is not merely to address individual instances of infringement but also to protect the long-term sustainability of the handbook publishing ecosystem and the legitimate interests of licensed customers.

### 19. Cross-Border Enforcement

The Engineering Handbook Series may be licensed, accessed, and distributed across multiple jurisdictions. Consequently, piracy investigations and enforcement activities may involve individuals, organizations, platforms, or service providers located in different countries.

Cross-border enforcement presents practical and legal challenges, including differences in:

- copyright legislation;
- contractual enforcement mechanisms;
- intermediary liability rules;
- notice-and-takedown procedures;
- data protection requirements;
- judicial processes; and
- available legal remedies.

Where international infringement is identified, the publisher should consider the most appropriate combination of contractual, technical, administrative, and legal measures based upon the circumstances of the case.

Depending upon the applicable jurisdiction, enforcement activities may include:

- submitting platform-specific infringement notices;
- requesting removal from search engines or online marketplaces where permitted;
- communicating with hosting providers;
- engaging local legal counsel where appropriate;
- coordinating with authorized publishing or distribution partners; and
- pursuing other lawful remedies available under the applicable legal framework.

The existence of cross-border elements should not prevent reasonable efforts to protect copyrighted handbook publications. However, enforcement decisions should remain proportionate to the expected effectiveness, available evidence, operational costs, and the scale of the infringement.

---

### 20. Privacy During Investigations

The investigation of suspected piracy should be conducted in a manner that respects applicable privacy and data-protection obligations.

Information collected during an investigation should be limited to what is reasonably necessary for legitimate investigative, security, licensing, contractual, or legal purposes.

Investigative records may include:

- licensing information;
- account records;
- entitlement history;
- audit logs;
- communication records;
- technical evidence;
- watermark information; and
- other information directly relevant to the investigation.

Access to investigative information should be restricted to authorized personnel or advisors with a legitimate operational, contractual, or legal need to access such information.

Personal information should not be disclosed unnecessarily during an investigation and should be handled in accordance with applicable privacy legislation, contractual obligations, internal security policies, and any published privacy documentation governing the Engineering Handbook ecosystem.

Nothing in this Section limits the publisher's ability to disclose information where disclosure is required by law, authorized by the applicable license agreement, necessary to protect legitimate legal rights, or required during judicial, regulatory, or law-enforcement proceedings.

The objective of this Section is to ensure that anti-piracy investigations remain both effective and respectful of the privacy rights of legitimate license holders while preserving the evidence necessary to support appropriate enforcement actions.

### 21. Internal Record Management

Accurate and well-maintained records are essential for effective anti-piracy operations. Consistent documentation enables the publisher to investigate incidents efficiently, apply licensing policies uniformly, and demonstrate the basis for enforcement decisions where necessary.

The publisher should maintain appropriate records relating to:

- license issuance;
- customer entitlements;
- document instance identifiers;
- watermark assignments;
- investigation activities;
- compliance notices;
- corrective actions;
- enforcement outcomes; and
- other records reasonably necessary for license administration.

Records should be maintained using procedures that promote accuracy, integrity, and appropriate access controls.

Where records are corrected, updated, or supplemented during an investigation, the changes should be documented to preserve a clear operational history.

Reasonable record-retention periods should reflect legal requirements, contractual obligations, operational needs, and applicable internal record-management policies.

---

### 22. Cooperation with Third Parties

The publisher may cooperate with third parties where such cooperation assists in preventing, investigating, or addressing unauthorized distribution of handbook publications.

Appropriate third parties may include:

- hosting providers;
- cloud-service providers;
- online marketplaces;
- payment processors;
- educational institutions;
- organizational customers;
- publishing partners;
- legal representatives; and
- law-enforcement or regulatory authorities where permitted or required by law.

Cooperation should be limited to information reasonably necessary for the specific purpose and should remain consistent with applicable legal, contractual, confidentiality, and privacy obligations.

Nothing in this Section requires the publisher to disclose confidential information beyond what is reasonably necessary to protect its legitimate rights or to comply with applicable legal obligations.

---

### 23. Continuous Improvement

The anti-piracy framework should be subject to periodic review to ensure that it remains effective against evolving technological, legal, and operational risks.

Reviews may consider:

- emerging piracy methods;
- developments in digital publishing technologies;
- changes in copyright and licensing law;
- customer feedback;
- institutional experience;
- investigation outcomes;
- security incidents; and
- operational performance metrics.

Where deficiencies are identified, the publisher should implement reasonable improvements to licensing documentation, technical safeguards, operational procedures, educational materials, or enforcement practices.

Continuous improvement supports a licensing ecosystem that remains effective, proportionate, and responsive while minimizing unnecessary burdens on legitimate license holders.

The objective is not to eliminate every possible infringement—which is neither technically nor practically achievable—but to maintain a balanced framework that continuously strengthens copyright protection, improves compliance, and supports the long-term sustainability of the Engineering Handbook Series.

### 24. Publisher Commitments

While this Manual establishes the responsibilities of license holders, the publisher likewise accepts corresponding responsibilities necessary to maintain a fair, transparent, and professionally administered licensing ecosystem.

Accordingly, the publisher commits to:

- applying licensing policies consistently and in good faith;
- conducting investigations objectively and based upon available evidence;
- respecting applicable contractual, copyright, and privacy obligations;
- providing clear licensing documentation wherever reasonably practicable;
- maintaining reasonable security measures for publishing systems under its control;
- protecting confidential customer information in accordance with applicable policies and law;
- responding to legitimate licensing inquiries within a reasonable timeframe;
- correcting administrative errors where identified; and
- continuously improving licensing, security, and anti-piracy procedures.

The publisher further recognizes that effective copyright protection depends not only upon enforcement but also upon transparency, consistency, and trust between the publisher and licensed customers.

Nothing in this Section limits the publisher's legal rights, contractual remedies, or enforcement authority where material license violations or copyright infringement are established.

---

### 25. Guiding Principles

The anti-piracy strategy established by this Manual is founded upon a set of guiding principles that govern the interpretation and application of every preceding Section.

These principles should inform operational decisions, investigations, compliance activities, and enforcement measures throughout the Engineering Handbook ecosystem.

#### 25.1 Fairness

Every reported incident should be evaluated objectively and without predetermined assumptions regarding responsibility or intent.

#### 25.2 Evidence-Based Decision Making

Material enforcement decisions should be supported by credible and verifiable evidence rather than speculation, inference, or unsupported allegations.

#### 25.3 Proportionality

The response to a licensing violation should reflect its seriousness, scale, intent, and resulting impact. Minor good-faith errors should not ordinarily receive the same response as deliberate commercial piracy.

#### 25.4 Transparency

Licensing rules, customer responsibilities, investigative procedures, and enforcement expectations should be communicated as clearly as reasonably practicable.

#### 25.5 Respect for Legitimate License Holders

Anti-piracy measures should protect intellectual property without creating unnecessary obstacles for individuals and organizations that comply with their licensing obligations.

#### 25.6 Privacy and Confidentiality

Investigations should respect applicable privacy requirements while protecting confidential business information and preserving legitimate evidentiary records.

#### 25.7 Continuous Improvement

The publisher should periodically review and refine technical safeguards, licensing documentation, operational procedures, and enforcement practices in response to evolving legal, technological, and operational developments.

#### 25.8 Sustainability

The ultimate purpose of the anti-piracy framework is to preserve the long-term sustainability, integrity, and continued development of the Engineering Handbook Series while supporting a fair and trustworthy licensing environment for all legitimate license holders.

Collectively, these principles establish the ethical and operational foundation of the Engineering Handbook anti-piracy program and should guide the interpretation of this Part wherever discretion or professional judgment is required.

### 26. Roles and Responsibilities

Effective anti-piracy operations require clearly defined responsibilities across the publishing ecosystem. Every participant involved in the creation, distribution, licensing, or administration of the Engineering Handbook Series contributes to protecting its intellectual property.

#### 26.1 Publisher

The publisher is responsible for:

- establishing licensing policies;
- maintaining copyright ownership records;
- implementing reasonable technical and administrative safeguards;
- conducting investigations where appropriate;
- managing enforcement activities;
- maintaining licensing documentation; and
- continuously improving the anti-piracy framework.

---

#### 26.2 License Holders

Every license holder is responsible for:

- complying with the applicable license agreement;
- protecting account credentials;
- preventing unauthorized redistribution;
